1117066037|5.0|For Whom The Bell Tolls by Ernest Hemingway<br />Book Review by Benn Bell<br />The title \\"For Whom The Bell Tolls\\" is taken from a poem written by John Donne wherein he makes the claim that because of our common humanity, every death necessarily diminishes each of us, therefore, ask not for whom the bell tolls, it tolls for thee. This is a book about death and dying set against the backdrop of the Spanish Civil War.<br />Hemingway's novels and stories present a certain kind of hero: \\"The Code Hero.\\" This individual lives by his own code and struggles gracefully and bravely against death and annihilation. Another consistent theme found in Hemingway is courage under fire or dire circumstances, whether it is in the bull ring, behind enemy lines, or hunting man-eaters in the green hills of Africa. Cowardice is particularly loathed.<br />The novel begins in 1937 at the height of the Spanish Civil War and takes place over a four day period.  The chief protagonist is an American named Robert Jordon who has been tasked to blow up a bridge behind enemy lines in the Spanish mountains. He is aided in this task by a band of guerrillas headed by Pablo and the woman of Pablo, Pilar. We also meet the beautiful Maria. A young Spanish girl whose parents were murdered by Fascists soldiers who then raped and abused Maria. Some say that Maria represents Spain and her gang rape represent the despoilage of Spain by the Fascists.<br />Robert and Maria fall in love at first sight. This is another recurring theme to be found in Hemingway, that humans can find salvation through romantic love. The couple makes love together in Jordon's sleeping bag on the ground outside the mouth of the cave where they are all hiding.  He asks Maria \\"Did the earth move for thee?\\" This is the earliest I have seen this terminology in print and is now considered a clich&eacute;, but it may be that Hemingway coined this usage.<br />Hemingway's use of language was controversial in this novel. Many Spanish words and phrases were translated literally word for word which gave a sense of the Spanish but sounds archaic and stilted to our English hearing ears. For example, the Spanish characters in the novel referred to each other as thee and thou. The traditional second person singular in English is \\"thou/thee/thy\\". The most exact way of translating \\"tu\\" from Spanish is \\"thou\\" or \\"thee\\". This was a bold experiment. Once the convention was understood and accepted one got used to and even grew to like it.<br />During the course of the novel many flashbacks and digressions take place as various characters tell their stories and reminiscence about the past. We learn, for example, that Jordon's Grandfather was a Civil War hero in the America's war of rebellion. We also learned that his father was a coward and that he shot himself to death with a pistol. It is indeed ironic that Hemingway's own father committed suicide and that Hemingway took his own life with a shot gun many years later.<br />Death is the primary focus of the novel with much discussion amongst the characters about what it is like to kill another human being, was it the right thing to do and what would it be like to die.  In the end, Jordon is fatally wounded after successfully completing his mission and trying to escape. He waits by the roadside while the others get away, hoping to kill as many of the enemy soldiers as he can before he dies.<br />It took me a long time to get around to reading this book. I have been carrying it around with me since 1971. I finally read it in 2011. Forty years. It was well worth the wait. While each man's death may diminish me to some extent, this novel has made me whole.literally word for word which gave a sense of the Spanish but sounds archaic and stilted to our English hearing ears. For example, the Spanish characters in the novel referred to each other as thee and thou. The traditional second person singular in English is \\"thou/thee/thy\\". The most exact way of translating \\"tu\\" from Spanish is \\"thou\\" or \\"thee\\". This was a bold experiment. Once the convention was understood and accepted one got used to and even grew to like it.   During the course of the novel many flashbacks and digressions take place as various characters tell their stories and reminiscence about the past. We learn, for example, that Jordon's Grandfather was a Civil War hero in the America's war of rebellion. We also learned that his father was a coward and that he shot himself to death with a pistol. It is indeed ironic that Hemingway's own father committed suicide and that Hemingway took his own life with a shot gun many years later.    Death is the primary focus of the novel with much discussion amongst the characters about what it is like to kill another human being, was it the right thing to do and what would it be like to die.  In the end, Jordon is fatally wounded after successfully completing his mission and trying to escape. He waits by the roadside while the others get away, hoping to kill as many of the enemy soldiers as he can before he dies.  It took me a long time to get around to reading this book. I have been carrying it around with me since 1971. I finally read it in 2011. Forty years. It was well worth the wait. While each man's death may diminish me to some extent, this novel has made me whole.

Everyone is assigned work at the age of twelve and Jonas is no different. But his work assignment certainly is. He's been selected to take on the most honored - and the most painful - assignment in the community. It is a work that no one else can do, except for the man who was selected for the work before Jonas - The Giver. He trains Jonas and prepares him to really understand and acquire the wisdom that comes from the work. Not only does Jonas remain brave and courageous throughout his training, but he goes beyond that and thinks of a way to try and save his community even if it thinks it doesn't need saving.<br /><br />Jonas' community has been developed into an isolated community devoid of free will and the people have been trained from infancy to accept it and to know no other way of life. Every December there is a ceremony held for each age group ages 1-12. Everyone in a given age group is given the same thing. For all the new children born that year they are assigned names; the eights are all given the same style jackets to signify their age group; the nines receive their bicycles; the tens have their hair cut in a particular style; the twelves are assigned their work. Everyone knows their place and the rules. Adults apply to be married and The Elders assign the partners. Those parents apply for children. They are allowed only one boy and one girl and those children are assigned to their parents during the ceremony where they are named. Dreams are scheduled for discussion in each family unit as a matter of course every morning. Feelings of the day are discussed at dinner. But soon Jonas learns that everything doesn't have to be this way.<br /><br />How would our real world change if everything was assigned and regulated? If no one was allowed free will, would we all be safer? What if we didn't know about free will - would our communities and societies be better? 0061703087|4.0|There's something about Michael Crichton's novels that makes you feel more intelligent as you read them. It must be the subject matter the novels are based on and the ease of description and explanation that Crichton weaves into the story. Crichton writes of things most people wouldn't think of. And yet they're not so far-fetched as though the ideas sprang from Mars. His novels are usually based on some type of scientific fact. Take, for instance, his novel Prey. In this novel we learn a little bit about nanotechnology. Nanotechnology is the science wherein small devices or structures are formed by using atoms and molecules on the scale of nanometres.<br /><br />The entire story spans only the length of seven days. Jack Forman is the protagonist and tells the story in the first person as events unfold. Jack's work background is with computers. At the start of the book we learn he used to manage a team of programmers before he lost his job. The programs they write are very specific. They write what are called \\"distributed parallel processing or agent-based programs.\\"<br /><br />\\"These programs model biological processes by creating virtual agents inside the computer and then letting the agents interact to solve real-world problems...For example, one of our programs imitated ant foraging-how ants find the shortest path to food-to route traffic through a big telephone network. Other programs mimicked the behavior of termites, swarming bees, and stalking lions.\\"<br /><br />Now that Jack is no longer working, he stays at home and watches his and his wife Julia's three children.<br /><br />Julia Forman is the vice president of a company called \\"Xymos Tech\\" where she is overseeing a project involving nanotechnology in a medical sphere. Small nano cameras are injected into the bloodstream where they can transmit pictures of the inside of the body. It is at this point that all semblance of normalcy between these two characters comes to a mysterious end. Julia's behavior becomes very erratic and uncharacteristic. She works constantly and fails to return home at a decent hour. She leaves too early in the mornings. Her mood becomes defensive and volatile. Even her physical appearance starts to take on a leaner and edgier look.<br /><br />Thinking his wife is having an affair with someone from her work, Jack accepts a job offer to work at the same location as Julia. The facility is located in the desert in Nevada. There he finds that some strange events have occurred at the facility. A swarm of nano cameras has escaped the building and is now malfunctioning outside in the desert. It did not respond to the electrical summons to return back to the facility when it first went missing and it has begun a self-sustaining existence on its own. It is also murderous. For all of its behavioral traits and actions, the swarm is \\"alive\\" and bloodthirsty. It can kill anything and anyone within a minute. And it continues to come back to the facility to try to find a way inside. Everyone inside the facility is essentially trapped. It's up to Jack to figure out how to disable and kill the swarm since the nano cameras were built according to one of his computer programs.<br /><br />There is one man, Ricky, who seems somewhat opposed to the swarm's demise. Why would he be so opposed to the one thing that could save all of their lives? And Julia's still acing strangely too. There must be something deeper and darker going on...<br /><br />Prey is true to Crichton's style and usual gripping performance. It is suspensefully thrilling! B000002AZ8|5.0|Vitalogy is one of those albums that, when taken as a whole, absolutely blows you away.<br /><br />Yes, there are some odd tracks here that don't quite serve as much of an artistic purpose as one would hope. But it's hard to say they don't fit on the album - in fact, I'd say the only track that truly doesn't fit on Vitalogy is \\"Better Man,\\" which, on an album filled with rage and paranoia, doesn't musically gel with the other songs. It's a great song in its own right, but despite the dark lyrics the melody is too soft to be perfectly at peace on this set.<br /><br />Take out \\"Better Man,\\" and what you're left with is an album bursting with energy, anger, and artistic ambition. On the surface, the album lashes out against fame and record companies. In more unskilled hands, these themes would leave the listener feeling unable to relate to the music and therefore quite distant from it. But, as with most Pearl Jam recordings, Vedder & Co. lend the songs a universal character with lyrics that can be interpreted in many different ways. In short, anyone who has ever felt anger at a world seemingly stacked up against them will be able to not just hear, but *feel* Vedder's primal and wholly satisfying screams in \\"Last Exit,\\" \\"Tremor Christ,\\" \\"Whipping,\\" and many more - and will more than likely have an unsupressable urge to scream along. Vedder's vocals are quite possibly the strongest of his career, thankfully beyond the \\"yarling\\" stage of Ten and before the period where his voice started to subtly weaken.<br /><br />The genuine anger and desperation throughout the album is absolutely tangible, and matched only in glimpses on other Pearl Jam albums. Very rarely in music is such passionate energy bottled and translated onto a recording so transparently. If your life is perfect, then perhaps you should look elsewhere for your musical needs. For the rest of us, Vitalogy is likely to tap into and bring out your deepest emotions that have been dying for a piece of music to represent them. Pearl Jam fan or otherwise, it's an experience not to be missed.therwise, it's an experience not to be missed.

1416583432|2.0|I like the book's concept, but after discovering several technical inaccuracies, I've lost confidence in the book and have stopped reading it. For example, the book claims that human eyes can process approximately 60 images per second, whereas dog eyes can process 80, therefore dogs don't see fluid motion as we do when watching television. The author's reasoning appears to confuse different ways of measuring eye performance in ways that seem to invalidate her conclusion. For example, to quote a certain website,     \\"[1]How many frames per second can the human eye see?  This is a tricky question. And much confusion about it is related to the fact, that this question is NOT the same as:     [2]How many frames per second do I have to have to make motions look fluid?  And it's not the same as     [3]How many frames per second makes the movie stop flickering?\\"  Let's assume her [human] figure of 60 frames per second is accurate for (1). By her same chain of logic, movies seen in the theater at 24 FPS or 50i PAL TV shouldn't appear fluid to the human vision system, yet they do.  It's possible that these apparent incongruities are a matter of insufficient context and explanation rather than factual errors, but many readers will find them confusing.

0060734191|5.0|I was first introduced to Garth Nix a little before my 12th birthday-when re-reading the whole Anne of Green Gables series every month was part of my normal book rotation. I didn't know what a necromancer was, much less why anyone would want to write about a well educated young woman fighting them to save her father and a whole Kingdom that had never really been her home.   Sabriel lives in a land divided by an old, crumbling wall. It's nothing special this wall, old and crumbling, nothing that would really keep out an invading force. Yet on the other side the Army of Ancelstierre waits with old fashioned weapons for something horrible to come over the wall. They wait in the dark because frequently the lights go out and any kind of technology fails them. They used swords instead of guns because the guns fall apart. All because of what is on the other side of that wall.  But there comes a time in every young girl's life when she must embrace the inner rebel who lives inside, just screaming to get to out. Sabriel's moment comes just after she graduates a finishing school in Ancelstierre that also teaches charter magic and martial arts-when a sending appears from her father giving her the bells and sword of the Abhorsen. Interpreting this as a call for help- meaning her father is either dead or trapped too far in death to get out on his own-Sabriel heads for the old Kingdom, a land she may have been born in but has no practicle knowledge of-and no idea of the new dangers abounding within.   Sabriel may be an Abhorsen-one of many in a long hereditary line-but this family of necromancers doesn't raise the dead to do their bidding, they lay them back down and send them back from whence they came.  Sabriel knows almost nothing about this.  Could she quell the tides of rising dead?  Interpret her father's message, travel through a world she has no preparation for with only a servant bound by the most powerful magic to keep it from killing her? Free a mysterious man trappedin a spell and bound by guilt for an act committed over 200 years ago that still has ramifications today?  Try to defeat an evil that has defied the Abhorsen for hundreds of years? And bring the old Kingdom into something back of the glory it once was?  As the terrifying repository of knowledge on all things dead says on its very last page: does the walker chose the path-or the path chose the walker?  I should mention that Sabriel can stand totally on its own and does so very nicely.  *******Spoilers next about what happens after Sabriel. *******  Next we have Lirael, the only member of the prophetic Clayr who can't see into the future and has dark hair instead of blond and sharp features instead of cherubic ones. Ostracized by her community after finally realizing she'll never gain the sight she becomes a Liberian in the Clayr's great library-which holds so much more than just books.  Working there Lirael becomes an accomplished charter mage and even gains a mysterious friend-the disreputable dog- who grew out of a statue and gains her freedom when she discovers her true destiny and identity.   It's been fourteen years since Sabriel became Queen/Abhorsen and in that time the old kingdom has become considerably safer-but there are still areas no one goes. Around red lake is one of them. And it just so happens that Prince Sammeth's friend from Ancelstierre, Nick (who happens to not believe anything about the old kingdom and that everything has a scientific explanation) is funding a dig at a site there that people avoid as much as possible and lighting strikes far more often than normal.  On the other side of the wall in Ancelstierre the King and Queen of the old Kingdom are politicking with the government so they don't sent thousands of refugees over the wall with promises of free land-when really they'll be walking straight into a trap set by a necromancer Sabriel has yet to catch.  And back in the Old Kingdom Sam is plotting an escape from his necromancy lessons-ashe is certain he is not meant to be the next Abhorsen. But his older sister is so clearly meant to be queen that the burden falls upon him. So does what any noble prince would do in the situation- runs away.   And lastly the whole Gangs here in Abhorsen to face the biggest evil of all- something so dangerous it was buried not only in the earth but in seven layers of warded materials guarded by the Abhorsen, sought by many a necromancer- none of whom knew what they were getting into. This is not just a fight to save a life or a kingdom, but the whole world and may more after that.    These books are amazing fantasy novels that manage to straddle the age gap between young adult and adult very well. They are quite scary, but that's the only kind of content that might be upsetting to younger readers. And since it's not nearly often enough you come across a real strong fantasy heroine who doesn't need anyone's help, they're also a big girl power boost. And funny and full of mystery and legend. Recommended to anyone over 12 or very advanced young readers who don't scare easy. I'm not exaggerating on this point- these books are full of characters and beings out of the worst nightmares.   Five stars. A trilogy to read over and over.

B004W2UBYW|5.0|Reading \\"Steve Jobs\\" is cathartic, as we are still grieved by his early death.  In one of his last acts of vision and greatness, Steve Jobs sought out the brilliant biographer Walter Isaacson to tell his story.    Isaacson describes his conversation with Steve Jobs when he told him he would not be happy with the entire book, yet he wanted the book written anyway.  \\"Why did you do it?\\"  \\"I wanted my kids to know me...also, when I got sick I realized other people would write about me if I died, and they wouldn't know anything.  They'd get it all wrong.  So I wanted to make sure someone heard what I had to say.\\"  This biography is beautifully complete and perfectly paced.  It's no hagiography, and at times it seems Isaacson overemphasizes his faults in order to balance the world's admiration for this incredible genius.  Steve Jobs, more than creating great products, created a great company.  He changed the very way we look at technology by marrying it with art: \\"It's in Apple's DNA that technology alone is not enough.  We believe that it's technology married with the humanities that yields us the result that makes our heart sing.\\"   He emphasized the importance of intention when talking about the iPod and the Zune, Microsoft's weak copycat product:    \\"The older I get, the more I see how much motivations matter.  We won (with the iPod) because we personally love music.  We made the iPod for ourselves...if you don't love something, you're not going to go the extra mile, work the extra weekend, challenge the status quo as much.\\"  This is what the world lost when we lost Steve Jobs- we lost our advocate for excellence in the technology world.  Reading this biography is a joy; re-living the journey with Steve Jobs is a delight.  Lingering in the background, coloring every word, however, is the ominous cloud, the fact that this man was taken from us way too early.  This sad fact was around every corner, and added gravity and importance to his accomplishments.  No ordinary man could have changed the world like Steve Jobs.  This definitive biography is at its best when Isaacson just lets him speak for himself.    He gives Jobs the last word:  \\"I've had a very lucky career, a very lucky life.  I've done all that I can do.\\"

B003L20IFQ|4.0|The concept of putting a man in a coffin for the entire length of a film probably sounded insane when it was first pitched, but viewing the finished product, one can't help but admire the brilliance of the writing, and the execution of the screenplay by director Rodrigo Cortes.<br /><br />Ryan Reynolds plays Paul Conroy, a truck driver overseas in Iraq.  His convoy is attacked, and he wakes up buried in a coffin.  Using his lighter for illumination, Conroy is able to see where he is, and is immediately filled with panic, but he finds that he is helplessly trapped.  He discovers there is a cell phone in the coffin, a device that becomes his lifeline to the outside world, and the device that facilitates much of what happens in the film.<br /><br />The writing is quite creative, taking a simple static situation and transforming it into a dynamic and dramatic one, mainly through a series of phone conversations, and a few text messages and videos.  On the screen for almost the entire film, Ryan Reynolds' performance is riveting, and nearly totally convincing, as his character learns that he is being held for ransom by some unknown terrorists.<br /><br />In a maddening series of phone calls, Conroy leaves messages for his wife, his employer CRT, and finally manages to contact a government agency that might be able to help.  Dan Brenner (Robert Paterson) of the Hostage Working Group is very familiar with hostage and ransom situations in Iraq, and offers a faint hope of rescue.  Conroy's captors, whoever they may be, are very experienced at the hostage game, as besides the cell phone, they have provided various articles for him to use, like glow sticks, a flashlight, a knife, and a flask of liquor.  As the ordeal goes on, these articles come into play and are used in various ways.<br /><br />There doesn't appear to be a breathing tube into coffin, so why Paul doesn't suffocate is something that you have to ignore.  Conroy receives a visitor in the form of a small snake, who slithers into the coffin through a hole in the side.  It's completely outrageous, but still electrifying, as death appears to be literally at Paul's feet, until finds a way to convince the serpent to leave.  The action is very much in the moment.  There are no flashbacks or fantasy scenes.  The only time another actor is even seen, is through videos on Conroy's phone.  Even so, the sudden death of a coworker still has all the impact of a blow to the gut.<br /><br />Conroy is intelligent, resourceful, and someone you indentify with and feel sympathy for, as he endures indifference, frustration, and soul crushing heartbreak.  The writing is excellent.  It's quite remarkable how dramatic the phone conversations are just watching Conroy's side of the conversation, and Ryan Reynolds' performance while lying in a darkened confined space, is just amazing.<br /><br />Dan Brenner's voice comes to represent Conroy's primary lifeline.  Unfortunately, although efforts are apparently made, the Government has difficulty locating him.  With the world literally closing in to engulf him, Paul Conroy is a man who is truly screwed.  You truly feel his anger and agony, when his caring employer calls back with some interesting news.  When artillery fire rocks the ground, rattling Paul's teeth, and nearly causing the coffin to collapse, the terrorists who put him in the ground are feared dead.  But there are still a few more surprises ahead for Paul Conroy.<br /><br />Shot in Spain, director Rodrigo Cortes' effort may not be uplifting, but it will probably generate some strong emotions.  Buried is a film that draws you into its world very close, making it more of an experience than most movies.  Although mostly on his back, Ryan Reynolds delivers a tour de force performance, while somehow managing to preserve his personal sanity making the film.<br /><br />Mostly shot in minimal or poor light, getting the Bluray edition probably isn't a priority, although the Bluray/DVD combo pack can often be found for a reasonable price used.  The Bluray edition does contains a featurette that covers some of the behind the scenes goings on, and things like how different versions of the coffin were used in shooting the various scenes in the film.on does contains a featurette that covers some of the behind the scenes goings on, and things like how different versions of the coffin were used in shooting the various scenes in the film.
B005974CNA|5.0|With the loss of Mike Portnoy what would become of Dream Theater? Well, though one Mike is gone another has taken his place and all is right in the family. Mike Portnoy is an amazing musician and will be missed from this band but with the insertion of Mike Mangini into the fold Dream Theater has gone back to its roots and crafted a piece of work that takes a turn away from the heavier, more metal sound that they emphasized with Train of Thought, Systematic Chaos, and A Nightmare to Remember.<br /><br />A Dramatic Turn of Events blends the sound developed over the last few albums and blends in the more progressive rock sounds of Metropolis, pt. 2, Falling Into Infinity, and Six Degrees of Inner Turbulence. The drumming was at the forefront of everyone's thoughts with this album and I have to say, though it is mixed lower than Portnoy's usually blasting stickwork, Mangini is brilliant, performing rhythms and techniques few could produce without making themselves the focus of the sound. John Petrucci is excellent as usual and Jordan Rudess has produced some of his finest work since Metropolis, pt. 2. John Myung's bass stands out more than I can remember in most of the songs unlike he's done except for a few tracks here and there while James Labrie's voice sounds better than it has in a while.<br /><br />All in all, an amazing production from the band and a benchmark for progressive rock and metal. Even if you've sworn off the band with Portnoy's departure, I recommend giving this a shot just to satisfy your own curiosity.

Here are some of my questions/issues:  Initially, why the remake? Not sure. The original film is also a very recent film and is available both dubbed and with subtitles. They are both period pieces in a sense, so their is no reason to update it. I initially read Matt Reeves pay lip service to the original only to go on and say he could do better (not in so few words). Seems like hubris to me. I honestly feel Matt Reeves never understood what the original film was about.  As a film, on it's own, it is just uneven. I know some reviewers complain that the comparisons to the original is unfair but truth be told, as a separate entity this film just falls flat, although not due to the performance of the two leads. The pacing felt off, their relationship felt forced at first and the aforementioned manipulation/sympathy dynamic just didn't play out as horror and I felt we were supposed to be cheering for Abby and Owen although we shouldn't be, as Abby certainly was no victim in this film.   As a comparison? The original lacks (as far as I know) any CGI work and everything is done on camera with minimal effects (***Edit*** Have re-watched the original, the cats attacking scene was CGI). Many of the original scenes play out and I really felt witness to the events unfolding before me. This version, the CGI stuck out in this supposedly straight forward film and the hospital burning scene felt like it was crow-barred in to have some action (although this scene is in the original, it plays out well as part of a sub-plot). I felt like I was watching a movie, which of course, I was but an intimate story like this should take you away from that point of view. This movie just didn't do that nearly as effectively as the original. There is some good here. Some of the scenes were very well done but, sadly, no more so than they appeared in the original which to say, I simply mean Matt Reeves few good scenes were just carbon copies of the originals. Finally, and already mentioned, Abby was no victim in this film. We are meant to see her as victim because she is a twelve year old girl, but she is clearly not through her actions and behaviors. Eli and Osker, at the core of the original, were both victims that found each other in a sea of garbage, poor Owen is just the next benefactor.  The main issue is (as I have read from other reviewers)that Matt Reeves changed the fundamental basis of the story. Not only did he make Abby far less sympathetic but the film tries to continue to create sympathy despite the fact that we should be horrified that Abby is continuing to bully/seduce/entrap Owen in much more subtle ways than the school bullies. Although Abby cared for her old benefactor, this is also another huge departure from the original. Without going into detail, Eli was as much a victim in many ways as Oskar was, unlike the Abby/Owen dynamic. While this makes a better 'horror' film, it ignores what the original was about, not to mention the audio/soundtrack cues really played against this.  Some of the logic lapses of the lone cop character were just silly. I understand Matt Reeves was trying to compact the story but it ended up ultimately hurting (in my opinion) it overall, making the film much more conventional, something the original film and book cannot be accused of.  Ultimately it is my hope that anyone interested in this film will seek out the original and be exposed to a far better interpretation of the novel and, in turn, seek that out as well.

B000IJ79WU|5.0|After the success of the original [[ASIN:B0012QVJXS The Superman Movie]], it should have been a foregone conclusion that Richard Donner would return as Director of Superman II.  In fact, most of the second film had already been shot by Donner when the original film was released in 1978.  Unfortunately, however, the producers (the Salkinds) and Donner had a falling out. Predictably, both sides blame each other for the split, but the generally accepted truth is that Donner was fired because the Salkinds felt Donner was to blame for the original film's budget issues and objected to his stubborn insistence that the Superman legend be respected; Donner refused to capitulate to the Salkinds' demands for various gags that would have brought the original film dangerously close to self-parody.  Thus, a messy divorce was perhaps inevitable, and Donner was fired in favor of director Richard Lester.  Unfortunately for Lester and the Salkinds, several major players refused to cooperate with the new director: Gene Hackman flatly declined to shoot any scenes with Lester, and Composer John Williams also quit in protest of Donner's firing.  To make matters worse, in order to get credit as director of Superman II, Lester discarded much of Donner's footage and re-filmed entire sequences.  Incredibly, the discarded footage included scenes with Marlon Brando as Jor-El.  To avoid having to pay Brando for his work on the second film, the Salkinds elected to discard the scenes of Jor-El in Superman II, and even removed Brando from the opening \\"recap\\" summarizing the original movie.  Any of these factors- a switch in directors, the cobbling together of scenes filmed two years apart by different directors, the excision of scenes featuring an important character played by one of the greatest actors of all time- could easily have resulted in a train wreck of a movie.  However, in spite of these factors- and, it must be said, to the credit of Lester and the Salkinds- Superman II was, by anyone's standards, a complete success.  Some fans and some film critics (Roger Ebert, for example) view Superman II as being superior to the first film.   However, many fans wanted to see Donner's deleted scenes and his overall vision for Superman II.  After years of petitions, Warner Bros. granted fans their wish, and we have Superman II: The Richard Donner Cut.  I view the Richard Donner Cut as the superior version of Superman II for the following reasons:  1.  *The return of Marlon Brando*  Marlon Brando's Jor-El was a crucial part of the first film- both encouraging Superman to be the hero that he became, and warning his son never to interfere in Earth's history.  Besides giving the first movie some needed gravitas, the scenes between father and son provided many of the themes that gave the film emotional resonance.  By contrast, I felt the scenes with Superman's mother (Susannah York) in the original version of Superman II, while emotional and poignant in their own right, never seemed to quite fit in with the themes established in the first film.  By restoring Brando and Jor El into the sequel, the film really feels like a continuation of the first film.  Brando gives a strong performance again as Jor-El.  Without giving away too many spoilers, I have to say that the scene in which the de-powered Superman returns to the Fortress of Solitude with his \\"tail between his legs\\" after rejecting his father's advice not to relinquish his powers, and is then restored by Jor-El, is one of the most powerful scenes in any of the Superman movies.  That scene alone is worth the price of this DVD in my humble opinion, and how the Salkinds could have rejected it to save on the budget defies belief.  2.  *A more mature tone to the film*  As much as I have always enjoyed the original cut of Superman II, I always viewed several scenes as a bit \\"off\\":  the slapsticky scenes involving Non and the invasion by the Phantom Zone villains of their first city on Earth, the goofy gags during the Metropolis battle, Superman throwing an \\"S\\" net from his chest, etc.  These Lester-directed scenes have been removed, and in their place we have a more threatening, sinister version of the Phantom Zone villains, a more brutal battle in Metropolis, and a showdown in the Fortress between Superman and General Zod that is now more cerebral and strategic (and thus, more interesting in my humble opinion) than physical.  3.  *The return of John Williams*  No, John Williams was not brought in to record new music for Superman II (how neat would that have been?!), but Editor Michael Thau (one of the unsung heroes of this project who helped bring the Richard Donner Cut to fruition) has incorporated the John Williams soundtrack from the original film into Superman II.  The results are nothing less than astounding.  After watching the original version of Superman II with Ken Thorne's soundtrack, I think most fans will be astounded by how Williams' soundtrack enhances many scenes in Superman II.  I will provide one small example: the scene with the astronauts on the moon when the first astronaut spots Ursa flying to the lunar surface.  The inclusion of Williams' soundtrack gives the scene a new eerie, mysterious quality that it lacked before.   In fairness, I will point out the two missteps that the Richard Donner Cut makes:  1.  *They took out the \\"General, would you care to step outside?\\" line*  A relatively minor omission, but Donner and Thau should have known that that line of dialogue is now in the Superman lexicon.  2.  *The removal of the patriotic original ending to Superman II & the re-use of the original film's ending*  Superman stands for truth, justice, and the American Way, and the original ending to Superman II where Superman returns the fallen American flag to the White House and vows to the President that he will not fail humanity again was PERFECT.  You can watch that scene a dozen times and it will bring a smile to your face every time.  (Minor spoiler) The Richard Donner Cut decides to re-use the \\"turning back time\\" trick of the original film so that Superman can erase the destruction caused by the Phantom Zone villains and Lois Lane's knowledge of his secret identity.  I realize that Donner would almost certainly have come up with a better resolution than the \\"Super Kiss\\" used by Superman in the original version to erase Lois' memory if he had been retained as director, but Superman's turning back time rendered the original ending at the White House impossible.  For this reason alone, I feel Donner should have swallowed his pride and retained Lester's \\"Super Kiss\\" resolution- or, better yet, simply ended the movie with Superman allowing Lois to know his secret identity.  At the very least, we still get Superman (posing as a regular human) getting revenge on Rocky, the trucker bully, at the diner after Rocky beat up the de-powered Clark Kent earlier in the film.  So is Superman II: The Richard Donner Cut perfect?  No.  Why am I giving it five stars, then?  Because I felt that the original Superman II was a five-star movie, and I think the Richard Donner Cut is even better than the original Superman II.  Had Richard Donner been retained as Director, and been given the opportunity to shape a proper ending to the film, I think Superman II could have ended up being THE greatest superhero movie of all time.  As it stands, Superman II: The Richard Donner Cut is a wonderful movie that should not be missed by Superman fans or fans of superhero/action movies in general.

B0041KKYGA|4.0|I always accepted the idea that Shakespeare wrote his own plays, and considered anything to the contrary to be merely speculation not fact. Then an uncouth illiterate actor, named Shakespeare steps in to claim the credit.  The peer remained anonymous for reasons of social acceptability.<br /><br />Another reason he may have remained anonymous which I totally loved was the parallel structure between what happened in the plays, and the real life events of the courtiers and Queen Elizabeth. Cecil, the courtier villain in this movie is a hunchback (historical fact), and brother in law of the Earl of Oxford. Richard 3 in Shakespeare's play is a hunchback, so the play becomes a social satire.<br /><br />A scene where a man is stabbed through a curtain mirrors a scene in Hamlet. A usurped heir is sent to Ireland, and there is a plot to kill him, similar to Rosencrantz and Guildenstern in Hamlet.<br /><br />Emmerich's direction gives Anonymous a much grander scope. For example it rains on the actor in the theater, as he recites his lines. Asked to speak to a crowd he stumbles inarticulately over his words. If Shakespeare was as inarticulate, and uneducated as portrayed, how could he have convinced anyone of his genius when he lacked the most basic skills of expression.<br /><br />If not Shakespeare, then who?<br /><br />I did some online research.  Apparently, almost two centuries passed before anyone seriously questioned Shakespeare's authorship.  It has been suggested that Sir Francis Bacon wrote these plays, but why would an already famous writer give credit to someone else.  DeVere appears to be the current favorite among conspiracy theorists.  If DeVere was excluded from the court, as he is in the movie then he would not be in a position to satirise the court, or include such pointed commentary in his plays.  DeVere as a child in the movie performs a piece from Midsummer nights dream for the queen. He could hardly have written it as an adult then, could he?Curiously, at times the movie appears to undermine its own premise.<br /><br />It has been suggested that Sir Thomas North, [[ASIN:146370366X North of Shakespeare: The True Story of the Secret Genius Who Wrote the World's Greatest Body of Literature]], wrote the materials on which some of Shakespeare's plays were based.<br /><br />He did translate Plutarch which Shakespeare used as a source in several plays such as Julius Caesar, Troilus and Cressida, Coriolanus, and Anthony and Cleopatra, adapting them for the stage, converting the words from prose to verse. According to this book, Shakespeare purchased North's works, and then adapted them from the page to the stage.  In some cases the corresponding passages in Shakespeare are word for word what was written by North. Somehow North did not get credit.  Rosalinde from As You Like It apparently is Elisa Nord, (north)North's daughter.<br /><br />Hamlet was adapted from a centuries old story called Amleth, and had several iterations, including Dial of Princes by North. Shakespeare made numerous changes to the original story  Amleth, making it way darker according to a book I read [[ASIN:0826480373 Seven Basic Plots: Why We Tell Stories]].<br /><br />In the movie Ben Johnson proclaims sincere affection for DeVere's language skills.<br />In reality, Ben Johnson famously said of Shakespeare: \\"he would... buy the reversion of old plays,\\"but then mark not whose 'twas first: and after-times may judge it to be his...\\"Epigram no.56.<br /><br />The movie claims no manuscript written by Shakespeare survives. If you don't have evidence that Shakespeare wrote something, does that mean you have evidence someone else wrote it?  You cannot infer alternative authorship from a negation, only from evidence. If you have evidence that these plays were previously written, and performed or were adapted from books, then you can verify that Shakespeare was not the originator, merely wrote a version, and credit the original source.  It seems that with some of these plays, they were circulating already, and then Shakespeare wrote a version, or made his own adaptation, which then became the definitive version.<br /><br />Undoubtedly, this movie will stimulate debate and controversy.  The idea that he didn't write a single word goes too far in my opinion, it would be interesting to know for sure what he did and did not write.  I do think this is one of the best movies I have seen all year.<br /><br />Wherever you stand on this,  I highly recommend you see it, consider it, and form your own opinion.  For me, it's too tabloidy to be taken seriously, the once virgin queen now a nympho, having an illegitimate son with the real Shakespeare, and so on.<br /><br />Even if like me you do not agree with the premise, you might be surprised to discover you still love the movie.<br /><br />I think you will enjoy it, and I hope this was helpful.conspiracy theorists. B000E1ZBGS|1.0|Ok, for starters, let me just say that I knew this movie would not be BBC's 5 hour long movie which in my opinion is the best version out there, and I knew that it would be much more condensed. I was fine with that, really. I teach British Lit in high school and I was really looking forward to being able to show my students a Pride and Prejudice movie, instead of just small clips.  But after the first five minutes of watching this, I knew there was no way I was going to show this in class. The list of reasons for that is so long, I won't be able to list them all, so I'll try to do a better job of condensing them than this movie did.  1. The characters themselves were not true to the book at all. Elizabeth Bennett is probably my favorite character of all time. She's lively, finds humor in just about everything, extremely intelligent considering she had virtually no schooling, and a natural wit that could rival any scholar. In the movie, she's a hissing, snarling bad-tempered thing with a chip on her shoulder. The writers seemed to take her propensity to find humor in others' ill behavior and twist it into making fun of just about everyone.   And then there was Mr. Darcy...ok, I knew going into this that I probably wouldn't like this rendtion more than Colin Firth's, but I had an open mind and was willing to give him a chance. And he wasn't all that bad, but paired with Keira Knightley's portrayal of Elizabeth, this movie just spun off into a ditch.  Mr. Bennett is a private, well-humored man stuck in a bad marriage. He is not whipped as he appears to be in this movie. The fact that the writers changed up a lot of the lines he had in the book to \\"modernize\\" the movie, did nothing to aid his character in the least.  2. The movie moves along way too fast. Again, I know it couldn't be five hours, but this was just ridiculous. The actors spoke so fast that even I, a hardened P&P veteran who could quote just about any line in the book had a hard time following the dialogue.  For instance, the scene at Rosings when Elizabeth, the Collinses (and Maria Lucas if her character had not been cut from the movie) are dining with Lady Catherine, the dialogue went so fast I almost got whiplash. Judi Densch had the potential to be the best Lady Catherine of all time in fact I was really excited about seeing her in this movie, but her screen time was cut so short, her lines so diluted that it's extremely easy to forget her. And believe me when I say that Jane Austen did not write Lady Catherine de Bourgh in a way that would make her so easily forgottten.  3. They cut out too many supporting characters, ie Mr. and Mrs. Hurst (Mr. Bingley's sister and brother-in-law), The Forsters, Mr. and Mrs. Phillips (Mrs. Bennett's sister and brother-in-law), and Lady and Maria Lucas. Again I know that time was a constraint, but all of these characters actually do play a vital role in the book.   4. And finally the BIGGEST complaint I have about this movie is Darcy's first proposal scene. Not only is not in Charlotte's Parlor and in the middle of Hunsford's grounds in a random gazebo structure in the rain (I guess to make it more \\"dramatic\\"), Mr. Darcy goes on to explain everything right then and there. Which I tell you right now is something Fitzwilliam Darcy would NEVER have done. He's too prideful, hence the title of the book/movie.   There was no letter, no suspense as the audience watched Elizabeth come to realize that everything she knew about Wickham was wrong and what everyone seemed to think of her family's behavior in general. One of my favorite parts of the book is where Mr. Darcy storms out of the Collinses parlor and Elizabeth is left fuming. And then there's the awkwardness with which he delivered the letter to her. Instead, I was the only one left fuming as I watched Elizabeth practically throw herself at Darcy after his explaination and by that point I actually shut off my DVD player and took a break. I could not believe that anyone could butcher such a great book so effectively.   In conclusion, this movie is HORRENDOUS! The only thing this movie did better than the BBC version is that characters are actually much more pleasing to look at, but that's it. Jane Austen is probably rolling around in her grave right now because these people just ripped her work to shreds.  And the worst part is, I now have students coming in every year that think they know all there is to know about Pride and Prejudice, but when they actually start to read the book they start asking questions like... \\"Wait, who's Maria Lucas?\\" and \\"What happened to the Gazebo?\\", and \\"Where the hell did this letter come from?\\" and \\"Wait, Bingley has another sister?\\" and my absolute favorite, \\"Why is Elizabeth so nice?\\" It's gotten to the point where I have to give a disclaimer at the start of each year, telling them that in no way shape or form should they watch this movie if they haven't already, and if they have they should forget everything.   Do not let your children watch this movie. Please. You'll be doing a favor to every English teacher out there. You want to watch a Pride and Prejudice movie that isn't 5 hours long, but still relatively accurate, watch the 1970s version or even the 1940s version. Acting's not quite as good, but they still top this movie about 100 times over. If I could've given this movie 0 stars and still reviewed, I would have.

0312576463|3.0|A short review, certainly not exhaustive.  The first 250 pages and the last two sections are terrific. The middle is long and seems like work to read.  Reminiscent of Updike's Rabbit books, Tolstoy and unalike unhappy families, and other sagas of time, this book shows through families that have disfunction that reverberates into next generations, through chosen social and political causes, and through sex, the many ways a free society's inhabitants can get it wrong or right.  What good is freedom if it is misused or causes manic clashes? What good is sexual freedom if confusion and objectification replace morality, faithfulness, and love? One man's moral quest to save the birds or worry about overpopulation or the environment may interfere with another's freedom to live his life by his own choices. The societal or political observations often get replicated in family interactions and choices in the book.  Bottom line - so far I think Franzen may be an optimist. 159420229X|5.0|I read this book on a recommendation from a friend and am extremely glad I did.  Joshua Foer had covered the US memory competition as a journalist and along the way met some characters that convinced him he too was capable of mastering the techniques used and was capable of competing as well as long as he spent the required time.  With strong self motivation the author embarked on journey of rigourous training, writing about the experience for the rest of us, and went on to actually win the US memory championship.  It is both a remarkable story as well as a highly informative read about memory and an overview of how it works.<br /><br />Moonwalking with Einstein is extremely balanced.  It is both entertainingly well written for the narrative and precisely stated for the sceintific.  I will go through the scientific briefly first.  The author goes through the science of memory throughout the book.  Humanity's use of memory has also changed substantially over time as a function of its needs.  In particular pre/post written word, pre/post printing press, pre/post digital age, all have had profound impacts on the need and use of our memory.  The author describes how our natural memory is far superior when it comes to our spatial and visual faculties.  People have known this for thousands of years and as a result figured out ways to use it to their advantage.  The author in particular used a method of memory dependent on using mental images of familiar physical buildings to place highly unique objects (associated with words that were the targets for memorization) and using the ordering of a path through the building as a visual means for recollection.  The description of his process and the science of memory are both instructive and highly informative.<br /><br />The narrative is even more fascinating than the science.  Joshua Foer is a journalist who by chance stumbled upon the US memory championships and in the chance encounter began an amazing personal journey that has no doubt propelled his career!  In the process of covering the memory championship he befriends Ed Cooke who helps propel him to decide to compete competitively and this book is a description of that journey.  One meets many remarkable and fascinating characters, from Ed himself to Tony Buzan to Daniel Tammet.<br /><br />Moonwalking with Einstein is a really refreshing form of journalism.  The book is easy to read, informative and engaging.  I found all parts of the book interesting and would have read the story or the science on its own but together it is a rare gem.  Highly recommended!

B000001GQY|5.0|In my opinion, Brahms's Piano Concerto No. 2 is one of the most powerful piano concertos ever written, and the Gilel/ Jochum performance really illustrated the immense tragedy, the sheer majesty, and the pure force of this masterpiece. Right from the start, when the first notes of the horn signal the tender first chords of the piano (which were played without any rush), I knew it was going to be an epic performance. Gilels plays the first cadenza as if every note counted, while other pianists tend to rush through certain notes/chords. Following the passionate cadenza is an equally passionate orchestra led by Eugene Jochum. If the first three minutes wasn't amazing enough, just take a listen to the rest of the first movement, where both the orchestra and Gilels ruthlessly drive you to the edge of your seats. You'll see what I mean when you hear the second cadenza, where Gilels starts off immediately where the orchestra left off, pounding chord after chord of pure frenzied passion, proceeded by a release of weight - giving you 2 minutes of relief, when suddenly he proceeds to rip through the chords with a demonic temper. Cadenzas after cadenza, let Gilels show you what this masterpiece is all about.

1609611543|5.0|Wheat Belly by Cardiologist Dr. William Davis, M.D., offers considerable insight into the potential for wheat based products to be a significant cause of both obesity and the early onset of Type 2 diabetes. Davis gives us a provocative look at how eliminating wheat from our diet can not only help us lose weight, but shrink unsightly bulges, and reverse a broad spectrum of health problems, including Type 2 diabetes.  Dr. Davis points out that two slices of whole wheat bread can increase blood sugar more than 2 tablespoons of pure cane sugar. The consequences of wheat consumption, Dr. Davis points out, are not just manifested on the body's surface, but can also reach deep down into virtually every organ of the body, from the intestines, liver, heart, the thyroid gland and even the brain.<br /><br />Dr. Davis explains that there has been a national trend to reduce fat and cholesterol intake and increase carbohydrate calories, creating a \\"peculiar\\" situation in which products made from wheat  have not just increased their presence in our diets, but have come to dominate our diets.  Davis points out that today we are told to eat more healthy whole grains.<br /><br />In wheat free experiments conducted by Dr. Davis and his staff over a 3 month period, blood glucose levels in diabetic patients had dropped from a diabetic range of 136 mg/dl or greater to a normal range. Diabetics were transitioned into non-diabetics during the course of the experiment.  According to Davis, diabetes, in many cases, can be cured-not simply managed- by the removal of carbohydrates, especially wheat.<br /><br />Throughout the book, Dr. William Davis goes on to explain that wheat, more than any other foodstuff, has been woven into the fabric of the \\"American food experience\\".  Davis points out that wheat as a crop, has succeeded on an unprecedented scale, exceeded only by corn in acreage of farmland planted.<br /><br />Dr. Davis points out that modern genetically modified wheat does not even closely resemble the wheat of our ancestors. The einkorn based wheat of our ancestors.  Davis even conducted experiments by baking bread utilizing einkorn in one loaf and modern whole wheat in another loaf.  He concluded that after consumption of each product, blood sugar was only slightly elevated by consuming in einkorn based wheat, while blood sugar was great elevated (70 points or more through the consumption of modern whole wheat.<br /><br />In the final chapter of the book, Dr. Davis offers a number of wheat free menus as a means of getting started on the transition to a wheat free life. He also lists a number of foods that Americans consume on a daily basis that contain whole wheat, although it may not be evident. This list include beverages,  breakfast cereals, cheese products, energy, protein, and meal replacement bars.<br /><br />The Appendix contains a plethora of wheat free recipes, ranging from appetizers to main courses, to desserts.<br /><br />Wheat Belly, by Dr. William Davis, M.D. can be considered a well researched and authoritative source of information on wheat related diseases and health issues.  Dr. Davis includes a list of over 15 pages of references, indexed by chapter.  Davis does a commendable job of correlating the historical evolution of our wheat supply and a corresponding increase in wheat consumption with ever increasing incidences of diabetes, obesity, and a host of other health related issues.<br /><br />The book Wheat Belly should be considered a must read for all physicians, Type 2 diabetics, the obese, and those experiencing undiagnosed  health related issues.  This book contains a wealth of information under one cover.. The einkorn based wheat of our ancestors.  Davis even conducted experiments by baking bread utilizing einkorn in one loaf and modern whole wheat in another loaf. Approximately 1/3 of the Irish population disappeared because of the famine. (The still existing good potatoes were sent off to England to be sold and for the landowners, speculators and traders to profit from.)  At this time in U.S. history, the newly arrived immigrants from Ireland were heavily discriminated against by the WASP population.  In other words, the same type of Anglo power structure directed both cultural and material negative feedback toward these new arrivals.  Is it any wonder many of them felt they had more in common with the Mexican soldados than they felt they had with their WASP commanders and WASP fellow soldiers.  They had escaped the Anglo-Irish oppression in their own nation to only face it again in the U.S.    When the San Patracios enlisted on the side of the Mexicans (who were fighting to protect and keep a large part of their nation - Alta Mexico), these men received nothing but positive cultural and material feedback.  In fact, to this day, the San Patricios have monuments built to their memory throughout Mexico. To the Mexicans, they are heroes. Walter Issacson's biography reveals that Steve Jobs was very often an incredibly cruel human being.  If he didn't agree with a dissenting opinion, rather than have an open, mature debate he would lash out and cut the other person down, saying something along the lines of, 'That's the f*****g stupidest idea I've ever heard.'  He would embarrass and shame people, often Apple employees working long hours to try and bring his ideas to reality.    Anyone who has worked for corporate America for a while has likely come in contact with, or knows someone who has, a really bad boss.  Steve Jobs was the epitome of a bad boss.  He was a brutal, mean and nasty BULLY.  It appears the best thing he would ever say about an person's idea was, 'Well, it's a start', implying it's not good enough.  If he did like someone's idea, he would not say so, but then suggest it as his own idea a week later.  Steve Jobs openly admitted to getting the ideas for the graphical user interface and the mouse from a trip to Xerox Parc, and took these ideas and made them his own.  Yet he harshly criticized Bill Gates for 'stealing' his idea and turning it into Windows.  What a study in contrasts.  A man to be admiredfor his brilliant creative vision combined with the ability to execute and successfully commercialize his inventions.  And at the same time, a narcissistic bully who lacked basic human kindness and decency.

B004W2UBYW|2.0|How does anyone compile the complexity of steve jobs--his personality, business acumen, spirituality-- into a single book? I do not believe it entirely possible, especially after reading through the 600 pages of this newest work about SJobs.  With few exceptions, this book imparts no new information---most of the content can be retrieved from old magazine articles, previously written books, and tons of Internet search results.  I also believe this book is not yet complete: it needs a serious edit to trim redundancies and to further balance out the hugely negative portrait with more enlightening information about this giant of a person. I often wondered if final edits were sacrificed to meet the accelerated publishing date. Perhaps a revised version will come through later, which I would advise people to wait for if they've not yet purchased the book.<br /><br />Nearly everyone (at least in Silicon Valley) knows that Jobs was eccentric, scary, tyrannical, histrionic, and abusive at times. So I was glad to read about some tender moments with his  father, sister Mona, his wife and children, his friendship with Ellison, and the times when he rewarded his engineering teams in his own special ways. I also really enjoyed the little stories about his friendship with Wozniak, and their pranks. I did not appreciate (and found it disruptive) to read far too many reminders that Jobs cried, screamed, and dissed just about anyone in sight. That message has come across from many other writers just as poignantly---in far more respectful ways. In fact, (other) better documentation about Jobs focuses more on his intelligence, vision, and marketing mastery rather than the (immature) emotion.  To regard Jobs as a rotten human being (which would be easy to do as  result of this reading) does not do him justice. His form of philanthropy did not follow the usual protocol of people from his level; his form of love and generosity differed from what we usually view as normal. 0679411585|5.0|Hal Moore's and Joseph Galloway's \\"We Were Soldiers Once...and Young\\" is a rare feat for a work of non-fiction: through phenomenal writing it brings the events of the November 1965 Ia Drang battle to life vividly and harshly. The writing and pacing of \\"We Were Soldiers\\" take the reader as  close as possible to the battlefield through Hal Moore's exhaustive interviews with hundreds of veterans of the brutal opening fight between US forces and North Vietnamese regulars, deep research and his own experience having commanded the American forces there. From the first page the reader will be drawn in and it will be hard to put this book down, as clichd as that may sound.  Moore and Galloway's \\"We Were Soldiers\\" is especially memorable in that--(for this reviewer it was the first time)--not only was the viewpoint of the enemy expressed, but that the authors interviewed the actual North Vietnamese commander they had faced at Landing Zone X-Ray. The insight provided by then-LTC An on how he met and battled his American adversaries helps to paint a fuller portrait of the fighting.  Finally, the authors took a rare step to examine the after effects of the battle, and the \\"after after-effects.\\" Moore and Galloway illustrate the true and rarely examined cost of war by interviewing the children of some of the men killed in the battle. The deep and devastating impact the loss of a father could have on the upbringing of a baby girl or a toddler at the time of the battle serve as a stark denouement to the story.

1451648537|4.0|This 600-page tome has something for everyone. If you like biography, you'll appreciate Walter Isaacson's skillful weaving of the threads he gathered from hundreds of first-person interviews and other primary resources. If you like innovation and technology, you'll welcome the inside stories on many of the products we use every day and which will no doubt influence spinoffs for decades to come. If you like business, you'll enjoy the behind-the-scenes looks at Apple Computer, Xerox, Microsoft, Pixar, and even PepsiCo. If you like psychology, you'll be enthralled by the bundle of contradictions who was Steve Jobs.<br /><br />Being a student of leadership and human performance, I found myself mostly wondering what might have been. Steve Jobs said he loved and appreciated the parents who adopted him as a baby. Yet by his own admission he often treated his parents with disdain, manipulation, and even bullying. Jobs said he admired people who contributed to the innovation and technology that were the driving forces in his life. Yet he was insufferably unkind and ungrateful to many of them, even to the point of denying them both the credit and the rewards they clearly deserved. In his own words, Jobs said he was gifted. Yet he claimed not to believe in God. This leaves one to wonder what - or Whom - he regarded as the source of his gifts.<br /><br />There's no doubt that Steve Jobs was a genius. He was visionary. His contributions to the way we communicate and conduct business will be felt for generations. But let's not get carried away with the hero worship we see in some of the commentary about his life and recent death. While not detracting from his obvious strengths, let's ponder how much more effective he could have been had he chosen to collaborate with others more generously and humbly. Much has been said about Jobs' famous reality distortion field in which he seemed oblivious to the limits of his own paradigms and the consequences of his own behaviors. It is possible (and advisable) to be tough on ideas and issues while being civil and humane with people. Let's not fall into the trap of assuming that brutality is a necessary companion to brilliance. It's not.o be tough on ideas and issues while being civil and humane with people. Let's not fall into the trap of assuming that brutality is a necessary companion to brilliance. It's not.

B000FCK8ZK|4.0|Fans of Showtime's \\"Dexter\\" owe Jeff Lindsay a huge debt thanks to his creation of Dexter Morgan.  Never has a serial killer seemed so sympathetic, so easy to identify with.  Season One of the famed Showtime series, now into its sixth season, comes almost entirely from this slim novel.  For the uninitiated, Dexter Morgan spends his days working as a surprisingly capable blood spatter analyst for the Miami Police Department.  Working closely with the detectives investigating Miami's nastiest homicides, Dexter's unique insight into the killer's mind has enabled him to solve many cases through unnervingly accurate hunches.   Why is he so attuned to the killer's mindset?  Because Dexter is a flat-out murderer.  Perhaps thanks to a childhood trauma (Dexter was found at 3 years old among the butchered body parts of his drug mule mother and her conspiring, ill-fate partners in crime).  His adoptive father, Harry Morgan, was a Miami homicide detective.  Harry recognized Dexter's need to kill things at an early age, and he made two surprising decisions.  First, he taught Dexter that some people deserved to die, so it was OK for Dexter to channel his urges if he was absolutely sure of his target's guilt.  Second, he taught Dexter how to cover his tracks.  And so it is that Dexter, armed with access to the Miami criminal database, seeks out the guilty and uses them to satisfy his Dark Passenger, the sinister force that lies inside his soul and demands blood.  All the great supporting characters from the Showtime series are here, albeit in somewhat abbreviated form.  We have Dexter's sister Debra, an ambitious cop laboring in Vice thanks to her beauty and inability to play politics. Rita, Dexter's unknowing fake girlfriend, is as damaged as ever. Angel Batista works a crime scene with bemused humor.  Fellow blood spatter expert Matsuoka tells horrible jokes and tries to fit in.  Lieutenant Laguerta leads the office with political skill despite the absence of detective talent.And the Ice Truck Killer leaves magnificently butchered bodies in his wake for Dexter to find.  Lindsay writes with poetic grace as we live inside Dexter's reptilian head.  Dexter, who claims to be unable to connect with humans, has a surprising gift for insight and observation, and his descriptions of the Dark Passenger merit several re-reads.  If you're looking for a thrilling, darkly comic insight into the mind of a killer, \\"Darkly Dreaming Dexter\\" is for you.

Do not let this dissuade you from watching this, but you should see the original as well.<br /><br />The original contains much less gratuitous violence, where as the American version seems to revile in it. This might be because an American perspective and expectations have demanded this of films for sometime. The Swedish film did contain violence, but in a way that left something to the imagination, and gave the general sense that the violence did not drive the story. A glaring difference in the sensibilities of American vs Swedish audiences accounts for there being much more nudity in the Swedish, which had to be worked around in the American. There is a point where having seen this almost shot for shot remake in the Swedish first, made some of the American dialogue seem pointless, were it not for the prior knowledge of what made certain lines pertinent to the story. There are other points however, that are made clearer in the American, such as the importance of puzzles, and who exactly the older man is. Either way, the strength of the story stands for itself, and can more than endure these glaring differences. The fact that the story deals with the mythology of vampires rather than out and out blood and violence is a fresh breath in the genre. The chilling aspects of this film are not what is normally used as a mechanism in most vampire films. The question of \\"letting someone in\\" is used to perhaps its fullest effect. Is one letting the vampire into their home, or to their heart? In the end, my opinion is that it doesn't matter what audience the screenplay is written for, therefore, see both this, and the Swedish. They are different enough to be entertaining so you can watch even though you know what happens, but you are rewarded with a different perspective.<br /><br />This is a film that will entertain you on the surface, but also challenges you to think deeper about what is really happening. 1451648537|5.0|This is a great book and ranks up there with my other favourite biography, Julius Caesar by Meier.  Jobs was a Nietzschean character with a strong will, ability, and a belief that he didn't have to play by anyone's rules. Perhaps he believed, being abandoned, that he owed the world absolutely nothing. His consolation was art; especially art through technology or engineering design. That's why I compare him to Julius Caesar, who was also needy, and sought consolation by achievement through military conquest. Neither man played by anyone's rules.  His lack of restraint made Jobs at times very cruel. For example, he made his parents, the ones who adopted him and scrimped and saved every penny to fulfill a promise that he would to to college, drop him off outside campus. He walked in, saying he wanted to be a footlose guy. But that doesn't ring true. Actually I believe he was ashamed of them. I know, because I was in a similar situation and felt the same emotion. But here's the rub. I didn't act on that feeling. It would have been hurtful and immoral to do so.  I don't believe there's such a thing as good people or bad people. We all have good impulses as well as bad ones. Sure we've all wanted to tell off someone, or simply walk out of a room rather than listen to a \\"bozo\\". But we usually think the better of it. Morality and respect for others is largely about making the choice to exercise restraint. Steve Jobs lived without external restraint, and it unleashed his creative will and energy, but it also made him a jerk.  It makes this biography a very interesting read too.  Issacson does a good job revealing this man, with all his faults as well as his virtues.  Jobs was a sensitive man, not a psychopath. He cried a lot. He was honest, if not viscerally raw, with himself and others. He left the world with great technological art. And he didn't manage to kill anyone, even accidentally (unlike Caesar).  So in the end, Steve, I have to say thank you for being here with us,and making the world a much more interesting place.

B00007KWHO|4.0|Yes' second record was recorded at Advision Studios in London between November 1969 and January 1970 with a release date of July 24, 1970. Jon Anderson wanted to bring in an orchestra for their second album but this didn't go over well with guitarist Peter Banks and after the recording sessions were finished, he was let go. The orchestra does tend to overpower the band in spots with Peter's parts, in particular, getting buried in the mix. The album featured two different covers, the EU one which is pictured here and an American one which featured the band including Steve Howe even though he doesn't even play on the album. The American cover is the back booklet cover in the Rhino remasters.<br /><br />The album starts of with a strong cover of Richie Havens on No Opportunity Necessary, No Experience Needed. Jon Anderson's Then is a strong song and comes across well with the orchestra being a bit more restrained here. Everydays is a cover of Stephen Stills' Buffalo Springfield song and gets a wonderful re-imagining here. The first half of the album ends with one of Yes' best known early songs, Sweet Dreams. The second half of the album kicks off in high gear with The Prophet, Clear Days and Astral Traveller are good songs but the orchestra gets a bit in the way, in my opinion. The album ends with a strong reading of the classic song Time And A Word. The bonus songs consist of a couple of single versions of Dear Father and The Prophet as well as alternate mixes of No Opportunity Necessary, No Experience Needed and Sweet Dreams. Overall, a very strong album but I prefer the first album a bit more.

This meditation takes place on two planes: on the Earthly plane, a nuclear family deals with the normal trials of growing up in a small 1950s Texas town; these human concerns are contrasted with existence on the cosmic plane, where natural forces, both macro- and micro-cosmic, lead to the eventual existence of the Texas family and provide a glimpse of what meaning their lives have in this grand scale, which will continue for billions of years after they are gone.
